## Common Behaviors with `SpectrumLike`

As mentioned at the end of the [reading tutorial](crate::tutorial::reading) `mzdata` uses the [`SpectrumLike`] trait to
define a set of operations that all data structures representing spectra need to provide. These are mostly implemented by
returning a [`SpectrumDescription`] instances. The [`SpectrumLike`] also provides a few compatibility methods to provide
limit interaction with the signal data, independent of the state it is in with the [`RefPeakDataLevel`]
returned by [`SpectrumLike::peaks`].

### Identifying a spectrum

A spectrum can be identified in an MS run by three coordinates, usually:

 - It's native identifier, given by [`SpectrumLike::id`], is a text string following a pattern that uniquely identifies a spectrum.
   Most spectra converted from a vendor raw data file will have a native identifier that follows a pattern particular to that vendor,
   or even the instrument model. The [`NativeSpectrumIdentifierFormatTerm`](crate::meta::NativeSpectrumIdentifierFormatTerm)
   type provides machinery for parsing registered native identifier formats, but there is no guarantee that a format is used properly or
   at all.
- It's index in the MS run, given by [`SpectrumLike::index`]. This is an ordinal value representing the 0-base position in the sequence
  of spectra that are in the MS run. It often follows a pattern mirroring the native identifier, but this only holds for full length MS
  run conversions. If you read in a file made from a subset of an MS run, there may be gaps between a spectrum's index and its native
  identifier. Sometimes, a scan's index + 1 is referred to as the "scan number", but this is only true for full length MS runs and not all
  vendors follow this convention.
- It's start time in the MS run, relative to the beginning of the MS run began. This value is always reported in minutes by `mzdata` but may
  be encoded in arbitrary temporal units in different formats. A common assumption made throughout `mzdata`'s time-based data access is that
  files storing spectra are stored in chronological order. This is not strictly required by all the formats being read.

```rust
# use std::io;
# use mzdata;
# use mzdata::prelude::*;
# fn from_path() -> io::Result<()> {
#    let file_path = "test/data/batching_test.mzML";
#    let mut reader = mzdata::MZReader::open_path(file_path)?;
#    let spectrum = reader.get_spectrum_by_index(15).unwrap();
    let native_id = spectrum.id();
    let index = spectrum.index();
    let start_time = spectrum.start_time();
#    Ok(())
# }
```

The [reading tutorial](crate::tutorial::reading#getting-a-spectrum) briefly discussed how to retrieve a spectrum by any of these coordinates,
provided the source supports them.

## Signal Processing with `mzsignal`

If [`mzsignal`](https://crates.io/crates/mzsignal) and a linear algebra backend are available, enabled by feature sets in `Cargo.toml`, `mzdata`
supports a range of common signal processing operations.

### Centroiding or Peak Picking

One of the most common things to do with a mass spectrum in profile mode, a spectrum with continuous signal data, is to discretize it into
centroids. [`MultiLayerSpectrum::pick_peaks`] and its variants, as well as [`RawSpectrum::pick_peaks_into`] and similar use `mzsignal`'s peak
picking machinery to do this. Note the warning that these methods do not update the spectrum [`SpectrumLike::signal_continuity`] value which
the caller must manipulate as appropriate.

```ignore
# use std::io;
# use mzdata;
# use mzdata::prelude::*;
# use mzdata::spectrum::SignalContinuity;
# fn pick_peaks_example() -> io::Result<()> {
#    let file_path = "test/data/batching_test.mzML";
#    let mut reader = mzdata::MZReader::open_path(file_path)?;
#    let mut spectrum = reader.get_spectrum_by_index(15).unwrap();
    // An error might occur during peak picking, or the spectrum
    // may be missing information, lacking the correct raw data
    // arrays, or the arrays may be malformed.
    spectrum.pick_peaks(3.0).unwrap();
    spectrum.description_mut().signal_continuity = SignalContinuity::Centroid;
#    Ok(())
# }
```

### Reprofiling

Sometimes you get centroid spectra and you need continuous spectra for another algorithm to work. [`MultiLayerSpectrum::reprofile_with_shape`]
and [`CentroidSpectrumType::reprofile_with_shape_into`] create new m/z and intensity profile arrays from existing centroid peaks. Like the peak
picking functions, these methods do not update the spectrum [`SpectrumLike::signal_continuity`] value which the caller must manipulate as
appropriate.

```ignore
# use std::io;
# use mzdata;
# use mzdata::prelude::*;
# use mzdata::spectrum::SignalContinuity;
# fn reprofile_peaks_example() -> io::Result<()> {
#    let file_path = "test/data/batching_test.mzML";
#    let mut reader = mzdata::MZReader::open_path(file_path)?;
#    let mut spectrum = reader.get_spectrum_by_index(15).unwrap();
    // An error might occur during reprofiling, or there may not be
    // any peaks to transform.
    spectrum.reprofile_with_shape(0.0025, 0.015).unwrap();
    spectrum.description_mut().signal_continuity = SignalContinuity::Profile;
#    Ok(())
# }
```

### Denoising

Spectra may contain structured or unstructured noise depending upon the data acquisition hardware
or software. [`MultiLayerSpectrum::denoise`] and [`RawSpectrum::denoise`] use a noise reduction
algorithm that detects local high density noise and iteratively subtracts it away, although this only
works on profile spectra.

```ignore
# use std::io;
# use mzdata;
# use mzdata::prelude::*;
# fn reprofile_peaks_example() -> io::Result<()> {
#    let file_path = "test/data/batching_test.mzML";
#    let mut reader = mzdata::MZReader::open_path(file_path)?;
#    let mut spectrum = reader.get_spectrum_by_index(15).unwrap();
    // Denoising may fail if the required data arrays are not
    // present or if the arrays are malformed.
    spectrum.denoise(3.0).unwrap();
#    Ok(())
# }
```

<!-- Uses spectrum idx 1555 from 20150930_06_AGP_Glycomics.mzML -->

Visually, this operation starts with the following raw spectrum, in this case from an Agilent 6550 QTOF:

and after denoising the same region of the spectrum looks like this:
